    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="796b11b79e684a249cd515ea281aa209">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f337a16d-8438-4f65-b349-4a26ca7c7a9e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

    Advanced Awards

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d112167853" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The development of sodium metal batteries has long been impeded by dendrite formation issues. State-of-the-art strategies, exemplified by sodiophilic hosting/seeding layers, have demonstrated great success in suppressing dendrite formation. However, addressing high-capacity applications (&gt;10 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) remains a significant challenge. Herein, the study revisits the interlayer strategy by simply covering a carbon nanotube (CNT) film onto the surface of a sodium metal anode, unlocking its overlooked potential for ultrahigh capacity applications. In situ Raman spectroscopy reveals the interlayer's fast-ion-storage feature, enabling deposition at the interface without capacity limitations. Consequently, in symmetric cells, one-year long-term reversible cycling and a record-high capacity of 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> under 90% depth of discharge is achieved, representing a significant breakthrough for stabilizing Na anode. Furthermore, the full cell with a 50-Âµm thin metal anode and a high-loading Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode (12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) delivers a stable capacity of 94 mAh g<sup>âˆ’1</sup> for 270 cycles (94% capacity retention).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202409062-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202409062-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-based batteries offer a promising pathway beyond lithium-ion technologies, thanks to more natural abundance of Na and low cost. Na metal emerges as an ideal anode material, boasting a high specific capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>âˆ’1</sup>) and a low redox potential (âˆ’2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0001_R_d112167845e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev. Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Na metal anodes face challenges like those of Li anodes but to a greater degree: the highly reactive Na causes continuous electrolyte decomposition, unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation, and uncontrolled dendrite growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0001_R_d112167845e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev. Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adma202409062-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0002_R_d112167845e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Lett.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This in turn limits active Na<sup>+</sup> utilization, compromises reversibility and cyclic stability, and even poses safety risks. Extensive research efforts have been devoted to stabilizing Na metal anode, such as creating artificial SEI layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0003_R_d112167845e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> developing electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0004_R_d112167845e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> modifying separators,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0005_R_d112167845e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designing porous hosts,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0006_R_d112167845e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. Adv. Mater. Energy Mater. Devices">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and controlling the nucleation sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0007_R_d112167845e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Sci. Adv. Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in most studies, a favorable assessment of Na plating/stripping performance relies on using thick anodes (300&nbsp;Âµm to 1&nbsp;mm foils), with low depth of discharge (DOD, typically &lt;30%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0006_R_d112167845e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. Adv. Mater. Energy Mater. Devices">6</a>-<a href="#adma202409062-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0008_R_d112167845e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A large excess of Na can compensate for the ongoing SEI formation and other irreversible Na loss, which leads to artificial performance improvements but is hardly realistic in high-energy scenarios.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, when addressing the high areal capacity of Na deposition, the community has appeared to favor a consensus approach to the use of sodiophilic hosting/seeding layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0009_R_d112167845e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These structures can help to regulate deposition, accommodate Na deposition, and reduce the effective current density. Recently developed sodiophilic hosting/seeding strategies have demonstrated to achieve long-term durability with over 80% DOD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0006_R_d112167845e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. Adv. Mater. Energy Mater. Devices">6</a>-<a href="#adma202409062-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0008_R_d112167845e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, one significant issue remains: the hosting/seeding layer typically has an inherent limited capacity to accommodate deposited metal without dendrite formation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1a</a>). This upper limit capacity is largely determined by factors such as the number of host/seed sites, microstructure, and pore volume.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0010_R_d112167845e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the large areal capacities (&gt;10 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) have rarely been reported in these strategies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0004_R_d112167845e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202409062-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0011_R_d112167845e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This limitation has undoubtedly become a bottleneck in the development of hosting/seeding materials. Overall, the Na deposition of high areal capacity (&gt;10 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) under high DOD (â‰¥80%) significantly exacerbates interface reactions, interface fluctuations, and dendrite growth. Undoubtedly, this presents a formidable obstacle for the entire Na community.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202409062-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ff707f1c-ce5c-4cb6-bffd-b2354c41c28d/adma202409062-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ff707f1c-ce5c-4cb6-bffd-b2354c41c28d/adma202409062-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24c41623-a5c5-45b4-b6d6-a2b6160dedbc/adma202409062-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ff707f1c-ce5c-4cb6-bffd-b2354c41c28d/adma202409062-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202409062-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202409062" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparison of different strategies. a) Schematic of&nbsp;the failure of the well-established hosting/seeding strategies under high-areal-capacity deposition. b) Schematic of the interlayer strategy developed in this study under high-areal-capacity deposition. c) Top-view SEM image of CNT interlayer. d) Scheme illustrating the spontaneous sodiation of the carbon interlayer and the corresponding redox reaction. e) Schematic of the ion transfer station.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In this work, we revisit the carbon interlayer strategy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1b,c</a>), where a carbon nanotube film covers the Na anode to achieve ultrahigh reversible areal capacity of Na deposition (50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> under a remarkable DOD of 90%) and superior long-term durability (over one year at 1 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> and over 2500 hours at 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>). Admittedly, the interlayer strategy seems a recurring motif in many metal anode protection cases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0010_R_d112167845e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there have been no studies investigating the threshold of this strategy, particularly in understanding the behavior of interlayer under harsh conditions of deep discharge and ultrahigh areal capacities (considered to be significantly greater than 10 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>). Through in situ spectroscopic observation, we have clarified that the interlayer requires a fast-ion-storage feature. Specifically, it includes two electrochemical signatures that cannot be decoupled, namely, the ability of Na-ion storage and fast ion accessibility in the Na inventory. And the behavior of this interlayer can be summarized as a fast â€œion transfer stationâ€, as shown in the schematic diagram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1e</a>), which will be discussed in detail.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202409062-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202409062-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202409062-sec-0030-title">2.1 Plating/Stripping Cycling Stability under Harsh Conditions</h3>
            
            <p>The long-term cycling stability was tested in symmetric cells, using 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme (G2) without additives. This classic electrolyte formulation can only enable stable cycling of symmetric Na cell under low current density and low capacity (0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> and 0.5 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). However, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2a</a>, under an elevated capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>, the bare Na foil symmetric cell suffered from voltage fluctuations only after 110&nbsp;h cycling. The interfaical instaility can be attributed to the internal dendrite penetration through separators, which can be further supported by disassembled cell photo (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2a</a>). It clearly shows substantial Na metal deposited on the glass fiber separator in the midde layer of three-layer structure. This observation is associated with the surface-growth Na metal,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0012_R_d112167845e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which passes through the Celgard separator but is impeded by the glass fiber separator. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image also revealed the uneven deposition at plating side, where a large amount of granular Na deposites can be seen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202409062-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a805318-31d4-48a8-8a3c-445844d7e0b4/adma202409062-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a805318-31d4-48a8-8a3c-445844d7e0b4/adma202409062-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6b29315f-8bf4-4e70-aff5-c8e68b232844/adma202409062-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a805318-31d4-48a8-8a3c-445844d7e0b4/adma202409062-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202409062-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202409062" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells. a,b) Voltage profiles of bare symmetric Na cell (a) and Na|CNT symmetric cell (b) at a fixed current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> and capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>. Insets are the internal structure of the disassembled cell, demonstrating the anode, cathode, and the 3-layer separators. c,d) Top view of SEM images of bare Na electrode after 200&nbsp;h-cycle (c) and Na|CNT electrode after 9000&nbsp;h-cycle (d). e) Voltage profile of Na|CNT symmetric cell at ultrahigh capacity of 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> and an elevated current density of 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>. f) Voltage profile of Na|CNT symmetric cell with DOD of 90%. g) The comparison of cumulative capacity under different deposition capacity with reported literatures. h) Schematic of the fully stripped cell (discharge curve in (f)) and photographs of both electrodes. After fully stripping, all deposited Na metal localizes at the Na/CNT interface. The electrolyte: 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In sharp contrast, the symmetric Na|CNT cell exhibits excellent cycling stability for over 9000&nbsp;h (over calendar one year) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2b</a>). The flat volatge plateaus remain constant and the overpotential increases by only â‰ˆ10&nbsp;mV even after an unprecedented one-year cycling (a record value shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2g</a>). The disassembled cell offers unequivocal evidence of this outstanding interfacial stability (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2b</a>). All three layers of separators are clean, with no traces of metal spots. The slight yellowing of the separators may be linked to the electrolyte decomposition accumulated during the one-year cycling. At the same time, the CNT film on the surface of the Na metal retains its well-aligned structural characteristics, with the accumulated decomposition product filling the space between the bundles along the axial distribution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2d</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The superiority of the CNT interlayer is more obvious at an ultrahigh cycling capacity of 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> at an elevated current density (2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2e</a>). Normally, 1 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> Na deposition is corresponding to the height change of â‰ˆ8.9&nbsp;Âµm cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> at plating side. Such high areal capacity markedly amplifies interface reactions, interface fluctuations, and dendrite growth. This capacity far exceeds current commercial demands, but it serves as a stringent criterion for evaluating the effectiveness of developed strategies. Impressively, symmetric Na|CNT cell exhibits a steady voltage hysteresis and long-term durability of over 2800 h. Its corresponding cumulative capacity (over 5600 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) ranks among the top documented values for this deposition capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2g</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0003_R_d112167845e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#adma202409062-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0008_R_d112167845e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202409062-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0013_R_d112167845e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, we explored large areal capacity under deep discharge condition (90% DOD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2f</a>). As discussed in the introduction, most reported works utilize thick Na foils, with 30% or less of the metal engaging in reversible plating/stripping. Therefore, conducting tests with close to 100% DOD represents an exceptionally rigorous regimen, approaching the threshold of interlayer strategy. In this experiment, a specific quantity of Na metal is weighed and prepared on one side (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information for details), while the other side is non-quantified Na foil, and both sides are covered with the CNT interlayer. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2f</a>, the symmetric cell demonstrates exceptional durability during 8 deep cycles at 90% DOD and 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>. After deep cycling, one side Na metal is completely stripped, and then the CNT interlayers at both sides were lifted from the surface of the Na foils (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2h</a>). Notably, on the fully stripped side, the clean current collector is clearly visible, with no residual dead Na observed. On the platting side, over 50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> of Na is deposited beneath the CNT interlayer, precisely at the Na/CNT interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0002">2h</a>). This deposition behavior indicates that the interlayer is distinctly different from the hosting/seeding layer. It can be considered as a non-host feature: rather than accommodating deposited metal, the interlayer conducts and homogenizes ions at interface, thereby inhibiting the formation of dendrites. Further characterization of the Na|CNT electrode at the plating side was conducted. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a> (Supporting Information), the surface morphology of the CNT texture is clearly visible, and in the magnified cross-section view (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information), it is evident that the CNT film did not thicken, demonstrating its non-host feature. More importantly, this also indicates that this interlayer strategy theoretically has no capacity limit, presenting significant potential in high-energy-density applications (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1b</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202409062-sec-0040-title">2.2 Fast-Ion-Storage Feature of Interlayer</h3>
            
            <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1d</a>, the Fermi energy difference between CNTs and Na metal drives the spontaneous sodiation of CNTs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0014_R_d112167845e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This spontaneous sodiation of CNTs can be achieved within 5 minutes, as demonstrated by electrochemical quantification in the half-cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Typically, sodiation of carbon materials bring about the spectroscopic changes of graphitic carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0015_R_d112167845e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the low crystallinity of CNTs makes it difficult to be detected, where a clear defect peak at 1350 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> can be observed in the Raman result (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the potential influence of this hidden sodiation behavior on Na deposition has been overlooked. To amplify this issue, we chose to replace CNTs with highly crystallized graphite. Graphite powder was coated on separator to achieve self-supporting interlayer (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3a</a>). This configuration also enables stable cycling of symmetric cell and deeply Na deposition (19 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). We set up in situ Raman spectroscopy as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3a</a>, where the Raman laser is directed through a hole on one side of the Na metal, enabling precise focusing within the graphite interlayer. Consequently, we were able to continuously record the spectroscopic signals from the single-sided interlayer throughout one cycle of stripping/plating (discharge/charge) process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202409062-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c5f55538-50fc-4fcd-a0dc-4734fc8151d7/adma202409062-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c5f55538-50fc-4fcd-a0dc-4734fc8151d7/adma202409062-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f974a34-5787-4317-9d19-a0bec9253cff/adma202409062-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c5f55538-50fc-4fcd-a0dc-4734fc8151d7/adma202409062-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202409062-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202409062" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Spectroscopic evolution and kinetics of sodium storage in the interlayer. a) Scheme of the setup for the in situ Raman spectroscopy; the laser was focused within the graphite interlayer through a hole on one side of the Na metal. b) Discharge profiles of graphite in different electrolytes with main salt concentrations of 1 and 2.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>. c) Na storage capacity of graphite in different electrolytes after contact with Na for varying durations (5, 10, and 30&nbsp;min). d,e) In situ Raman spectra of graphite interlayer in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> (d) and 2.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> (e) electrolytes, covering the range from 1400 to 1800&nbsp;cm<sup>â€“1</sup> during the first charge/discharge, with magnified views provided at selected timepoints. f) Voltage profiles of different Na|C symmetric cells at a fixed current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> and capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>. g) Diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub>+) of CNT and graphite tested in Na || carbon half cells. h) Tafel curves of symmetrical cells recorded at a scan rate of 50 â€‰mVâ€‰sâ»Â¹ between âˆ’0.3 and 0.3 â€‰V. The electrolyte: 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 electrolyte, the initial state (â‘ ) exhibits a peak at 1604 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup>, which is a characteristic peak of fully sodiated graphite<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0016_R_d112167845e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3d</a>). Despite the absence of an electrochemical process at this point, the difference in Fermi energy renders this process thermodynamically viable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1d</a>). As the Na stripping proceeds, the 1604 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> peak remains predominant until the discharge ends (part of Na is stripped from the characterized side) (â‘¡), at which point a 1580 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> peak emerges. The appearance of this graphitic carbon peak (1580 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup>) comes from the non-fully sodiated graphite,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0016_R_d112167845e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which means some Na ions are released from the graphite. Nevertheless, with the onset of Na plating, the peak at 1580 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> quickly diminishes, and the sole peak at 1604 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> becomes dominant. By the end of plating, the signal returns to the characteristics of fully sodiated graphite (â‘¢).</p>
            
            <p>Evidently, the sodium ion storage in the graphite interlayer undergoes a cycle of release/refill during the stripping/plating processes. This ion behavior can be summarized as an â€œion transfer stationâ€ (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0001">1e</a>). This behavior endows the interlayer non-host feature, enabling the Na deposition at the Na/carbon interface. Additionally, the spectroscopic evolution observed throughout this process offers empirical support for preliminary hypotheses regarding interlayer dynamics in Li metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0014_R_d112167845e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>On the other hand, the appearance of peaks indicative of non-fully sodiation at the end of the discharge (â‘¡) indicates a deficient Na inventory within the â€œion transfer stationâ€. This also suggests that the deposition behavior at the Na/carbon interface is likely regulated by the Na-ion storage behavior within the â€œion transfer stationâ€. Specifically, the interlayer's storage capacity and releasing/refilling rate are crucial factors influencing the deposition at the interface.</p>
            
            <p>To validate this hypothesis, we analyzed two distinct scenarios:</p>
            
            <p>Case One: Reducing the Na inventory within the interlayer. A facile method was employed to manipulate Na storage behavior, where increasing the salt concentration to 2.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> significantly reduced the amount of Na-ion stored in graphite (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0017_R_d112167845e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, a distinct difference can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3e</a>, where the initial stage (â‘£) shows the coexistence of 1580 and 1600 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> peaks, indicative of partial sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0016_R_d112167845e833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The coexisting peaks persist through the Na stripping. Notably, the graphitic carbon peak (1580 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup>) emerges predominantly at the end of Na stripping, while the sodiated graphite peak (1600 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup>) clearly weakens (â‘¤). This shift signals a serious deficiency in Na ion storage within the interlayer. And this deficiency is recovered upon the end of Na plating, evidenced by the resurgence of the 1604 cm<sup>âˆ’1</sup> peak.</p>
            
            <p>Overall, the 2.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> electrolyte exhibits a pronounced Na-ion storage deficiency within the graphite interlayer. From a thermodynamic perspective, any non-fully sodiated graphite naturally drives Na ions to replenish itself (i.e. the spontaneous sodiation), but evidently, this process is time-consuming. By employing electrochemical methods, we quantified the Na-ion storage capacity within graphite over different durations (see details in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3c</a>, in the 2.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> electrolyte, the Na-ion storage capacity is time-dependent, indicating that the deficiency of Na ion inventory within interlayer requires more time to be recovered. In contrast, in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> electrolyte, the rapid Na-ion storage behavior show no time-dependence. This result explains well why the Raman spectroscopic signals in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> electrolyte are predominantly characteristic of sodiated graphite, i.e., Na ions can be spontaneously refilled quickly.</p>
            
            <p>When addressing the difference in Na-ion storage rates within the interlayer, our attention turns to:</p>
            
            <p>Case Two: Rapid Na-ion storage. In the case of CNTs interlayer, its intrinsic Na storage capacity is lower than that of graphite (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). However, under a faster deposition condition (1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>), it exhibits more stable deposition behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3f</a>). Although graphite interlayer can cycle steadily at 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information), it suffers from the short-circuit at an elevated current. The dendrite formation suggests the failure of â€œion transfer stationâ€ to homogenize ion flow at Na/carbon interface. The faster Na storage behavior of CNTs is also evidenced in half-cells, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0003">3g</a>, where the ion diffusion coefficient in CNTs is several times larger than that in graphite, demonstrating their favorable suitability for rapid sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0016_R_d112167845e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, in the Tafel curves of symmetric Na|carbon batteries, CNTs interlayer demonstrates a higher exchange current density, indicating faster interface deposition kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0018_R_d112167845e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, we attempted to replace graphite with few-layer reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to improve interfacial ion transfer, as this material has been shown to possess faster intercalation kinetics in our earlier study.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0016_R_d112167845e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, although rGO interlayer proved effective in deep Na deposition and cycling under low current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information), it exhibited interfacial instability under larger current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information). This phenomenon is likely due to the slower intercalation kinetics of sodium storage in layered materials compared to the adsorption-dominated storage kinetics in CNTs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0019_R_d112167845e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Overall, rapid Na-ion storage compensates for the weakness of CNTs in Na-ion storage capacity. These results clearly emphasize how important it is for the optimal interlayers to possess a fast-ion-storage feature. It includes two electrochemical signatures that cannot be decoupled, namely, the ability of Na-ion storage and fast ion accessibility in the Na inventory.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202409062-sec-0050-title">2.3 Full Cell Evaluation under Harsh Conditions</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the effectiveness of the interlayer strategy, full cells were assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and Na Prussian blue (PB) cathodes, respectively. For high-voltage PB, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 electrolyte containing NaNO<sub>3</sub> additive is selected, considering the recent widespread application of anion additives in high-voltage alkali metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0020_R_d112167845e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Cell Rep. Phys. Sci. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Na|CNT || NVP cell, harsh conditions of high cathode loading (12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>, corresponding to 1.33 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) and thin metal anode (50-Âµm foil) were employed (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4a</a>). The cell shows a discharge capacity of 94 mAh g<sup>âˆ’1</sup> with a high capacity retention of 93.96% (at 0.5C) after 270 cycles under such harsh conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4b</a>). However, the bare Na anode without CNT interlayer failed at 25th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4b</a>), likely due to an internal short circuit (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4c</a> displays the charge/discharge profiles of the Na|CNT || NVP cell, where the polarization remains almost unchanged during the 0.5C long cycle. The rate capability is further assessed by increasing the current density from 0.1C to 2C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4d</a>), with only a slight increase in polarization observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4e</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202409062-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/12bcea3d-fbb5-47f2-acda-185d3a9561dc/adma202409062-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/12bcea3d-fbb5-47f2-acda-185d3a9561dc/adma202409062-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/04b4f159-daad-4f56-8c0d-abaa9a069147/adma202409062-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/12bcea3d-fbb5-47f2-acda-185d3a9561dc/adma202409062-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202409062-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202409062" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Na||cathodes full cells paired with NVP and PB. a) Schematic illustration of Na||NVP full cell with 50-Âµm thin Na metal anode. b) Long-term cycling stability of Na||NVP full cell obtained at a current density of 0.5 C. c) Galvanostatic chargeâ€“discharge profiles of Na||NVP at 0.5C (100th, and 250th cycles). d) Rate capability of Na||NVP measured from 0.1 to 2 C. e) Galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles of Na||NVP obtained at varying C-rate. f) Long-term cycling stability of Na||PB cell obtained at a current density of 0.5 C. g) Galvanostatic chargeâ€“discharge profiles of Na||PB at 0.5C (10th, and 300th cycles). The electrolyte used for 4.3&nbsp;V cut-off voltage: 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme with 50&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaNO<sub>3</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Moreover, under conventional excess Na condition (thick Na foil in the anode side) and limited PB cathode loading (1.1&nbsp;mg cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>), the Na|CNT || PB cell demonstrated a 1000-cycle long-term stability, whereas the bare Na || PB cell suffered from a sudden failure at â‰ˆ350th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4f</a>). This failure is also associated with interface instability. The sustained decrease in Coulombic Efficiency (CE) before sudden failure suggests that despite adequate Na inventory to compensate for irreversible Na losses, the continuous interface side reactions remain catastrophically detrimental to stable Na-based battery cycling. The increase in polarization observed in the Na || PB cell further underscores this issue (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202409062-fig-0004">4g</a>). Meanwhile, these results highlight the superiority of the interlayer in both interface stability and fast ion transport.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202409062-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202409062-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have revisited the interlayer strategy by applying a CNT film onto Na metal anode, resulting in stable dendrite-free cycling in Na metal batteries at ultrahigh areal capacity (50 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) with high Na utilization (90% DOD). This breakthrough surpasses the capacity limitations of well-developed sodiophilic hosting/seeding layers and other currently developed strategies. Through in situ Raman spectroscopy, we uncovered the â€œion transfer stationâ€ behavior and fast-ion-storage feature of the interlayer. This study offers a promising solution for stabilizing highly reactive Na metal anodes, effectively mitigating interface reactions, and suppressing dendrite growth. Nevertheless, given the limitations of laboratory-scale conditions, further investigation into thinner (10â€“30&nbsp;Âµm) Na metal anodes and higher-loading (&gt;3 mAh cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) cathodes is necessary for the development of commercially viable, high-energy-density sodium metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202409062-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202409062-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202409062-sec-0100-title"> Preparation of the Na|CNT Electrode</h4>
                  
                  <p>A freestanding CNT film with an aligned structure was provided by Prof. Shoushan Fan's group at Tsinghua University (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The specific surface area of the CNT film was 118 m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>âˆ’1</sup>. Solid metallic Na chunks were sourced from Simga-Aldrich. Na foils were carefully fabricated using a homemade mold to ensure control of thickness and diameter (typically 12&nbsp;mm) in an argon gas-filled glove box (with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> content below 0.1&nbsp;ppm). Quantification of the Na foils utilized in deep-discharge test was achieved through weighing (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information for details). The surface of the Na foils was thoroughly cleaned by scraping until achieving a shiny metallic appearance. The Na foil was first wetted with 20&nbsp;ÂµL of electrolyte, followed by placing the CNT film on top. An additional 20&nbsp;ÂµL of electrolyte was added to ensure complete immersion of the CNT film. The obtained Na|CNT electrode was then directly assembled into the coin cell under a fixed pressure.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202409062-sec-0110-title"> Electrolyte and Electrode Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>NaPF<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode materials were purchased from Canrd; NaNO<sub>3</sub> and diglyme (G2) were obtained from Sigma-Aldrich. G2 solvent was dried over 4 Ã… molecular sieves. Prussian Blue material was synthesized according to the reported literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202409062-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202409062-bib-0021_R_d112167845e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Res.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The laminate of medium-high/high loading NVP electrodes (â‰ˆ6.5/â‰ˆ12&nbsp;mg active material cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) and PB electrodes (â‰ˆ1.1&nbsp;mg active material cm<sup>âˆ’2</sup>) were prepared by casting a slurry mixture containing 94&nbsp;wt.% active material, 3&nbsp;wt.% carbon black (Super P, Canrd), and 3&nbsp;wt.% polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, Canrd) in N-methylpyrrolidone onto an Al foil. After drying, the electrodes were cut into discs with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202409062-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Na stripping/plating performance was studied in symmetric cells (CR2032-type coin cell) containing Na|carbon electrodes or bare Na metal electrodes. The electrolyte used was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2. Each coin cell received 100&nbsp;ÂµL electrolyte. The separators used consisted of polypropylene (PP, Celgard 2400) and glass fiber (GF/A, Whatman), arranged in a PP/GF/PP configuration within the cells. PP separators were chosen for non-destructive morphological characterization of the surface of bare Na metal electrodes. The cells were rested for 10 h before testing. In Na||NVP and Na||PB cells, one-piece PP separator and 70&nbsp;ÂµL of electrolyte were used. For the high cutoff-voltage PB cathode, a 50&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaNO<sub>3</sub> additive was added in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2. Galvanostatic cycling test was conducted on a standard eight-channel battery tester (CT2001A, Wuhan LAND Electronics Co., Ltd.) at room temperature. The rate performance test was performed on a NEWARE Battery Test System (CT-4008T-5V10mA-164, Shenzhen, China) at room temperature. Tafel plots were recorded using a Biologic VMP3 electrochemical workstation at a scan rate of 50&nbsp;mV s<sup>âˆ’1</sup> between âˆ’0.3 and 0.3&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202409062-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202409062-sec-0130-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Raman spectroscopy was performed by HORIBA LabRAM HR800 spectrometer using argon laser with wavelength of 532&nbsp;nm. X-ray diffraction was conducted on Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu KÎ± (Î» = 0.154&nbsp;nm). Morphological characterization was conducted using SEM (HITACHI SU8010, Tokyo, Japan). Prior to performing SEM analysis of cycled electrodes, cells were disassembled in the glovebox, and the electrodes obtained were rinsed with G2 to remove any residue salts.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adma202409062-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span><span class="author">Y. Jiang</span>, <span class="author">Y. Yang</span>, <span class="author">F. X. Ling</span>, <span class="author">G. X. Lu</span>, <span class="author">F. Y. Huang</span>, <span class="author">X. Y. Tao</span>, <span class="author">S. F. Wu</span>, <span class="author">X. L. Mater.</i> <span class="pubYear">2022</span>,  <span class="vol">34</span>, 2109439.
